[2025-10-26T18:35:14Z] Akshay: 1. Dating back as early as the 8th century BCE (matter of much debate), the X Sutras are considered appendices to the Srautasutras, themselves part of the Kalpasutra books, which are considered one of the  ‘Vedaangas’ or ancillaries to the Vedas. ↵ • The X may be considered construction manuals, with detailed design instructions and geometrical calculations to construct different kinds of Y, something regularly used in Hindu rituals. For this reason, it is also considered related to the Yajur Vedas and used by Hindu communities all over the world. ↵ •Today we have surviving manuscripts of four X Sutras. One of these, by Z, is considered to have earliest references to the Pythagoras Theorem. Z is also believed to be the guru of the grammarian Panini. ↵ • The name X comes from the Sanskrit word for measurement and literally means ‘the Rule of the Cord’. The word X is sometimes used in Hindi for Y, a nod to the contents of the X Sutras. Interestingly, X is strikingly similar to the Hindi word for Copper (the element).

[2025-10-26T18:43:26Z] Naman Maheshwari: Here’s how this breaks down: ↵ * **X** → **Śulba Sūtras (Sulba Sutras)** ↵ * **Y** → **altars (vedis)** ↵ * **Z** → **Baudhāyana** ↵ Putting it all together: ↵ Dating back as early as the **8th century BCE** (though debated), the **Śulba Sūtras** are appendices to the **Śrauta Sūtras**, themselves part of the **Kalpa Sūtra** texts, which are one of the six **Vedāṅgas** (ancillaries to the Vedas). ↵ * The **Śulba Sūtras** may be considered construction manuals, containing detailed design rules and geometrical calculations to build various types of **altars (vedis)** used in **Vedic rituals**. Because of this, they are closely related to the **Yajur Veda** and are still referenced by Hindu communities worldwide. ↵ * Of the four surviving **Śulba Sūtras**, the one by **Baudhāyana** includes one of the **earliest known statements of the Pythagorean Theorem**. Baudhāyana is also believed to have been the **teacher of Pāṇini**, the great Sanskrit grammarian. ↵ * The term **Śulba** comes from the Sanskrit word meaning **“measurement cord”**, literally **“rule of the cord.”** In Hindi, *śulb* or *śulba* can refer to **a measuring cord or altar**, and interestingly, the word resembles the Hindi term for **copper (tāmba)**—perhaps coincidentally echoing the copper cords once used in measurements. ↵ *

Along with Euler, Y however derived solutions for a certain class of 3 body problems,  called the circularly restricted 3 body problems. This in turn is the origin for the term Y points, which are essentially points between two bodies where the net gravitational force is zero, and hence used to position satellites and other probes. [2025-10-26T19:53:17Z] Akshay: This integral of the secant is super easy, any high school student can get it. However, before the invention of calculus, this was one of the longest ever unsolved problems in integration. This problem originated in the late 15th century, when X was trying to make certain tools Y to aid navigation, coinciding with Columbus setting off for the new world and the beginnings of the age of exploration. X had to use crude numerical approximations, and hence because of his painstaking work on the same, the integral is literally named after him as the X integral. Where is this integral used? for something you and I see every single day and a loot on this group. ‎image omitted

[2025-10-26T20:07:08Z] Naman Agarwal: The mathematical notion of limit and the closely related notion of convergence are central to any modern definition of integration. In the 19th century, Karl Weierstrass developed the rigorous epsilon-delta definition of a limit, which is still accepted and used by mathematicians today. He built on previous but non-rigorous work by Augustin Cauchy, who had used the non-standard notion of infinitesimally small numbers, today rejected in standard mathematical analysis. Before Cauchy, Bernard Bolzano had laid the fundamental groundwork of the epsilon-delta definition. ↵ X followed up on this by formalizing what is now called the X integral. To define this integral, one fills the area under the graph with smaller and smaller rectangles and takes the limit of the sums of the areas of the rectangles at each stage. For some functions, however, the total area of these rectangles does not approach a single number. Thus, they have no X integral. ↵ Who is X, you will also know him for a famous unsolved problem?
